	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Peak District</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District_National_Park&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Peak District National Park">Peak District National Park</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Upland area in England</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="fn org" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold">Peak District National Park</th></tr><tr><td <p>The <b>Peak District</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Highland" title="Highland">upland</a> area in England at the southern end of the <a href="/wiki/Pennines" title="Pennines">Pennines</a>. It is mostly in northern <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a>, but also includes parts of <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester" title="Greater Manchester">Greater Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire" title="South Yorkshire">South Yorkshire</a>. An area of great diversity, it is mostly split into the <a href="/wiki/Dark_Peak" title="Dark Peak">Dark Peak</a>, where most of the <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorland</a> is found and the geology is <a href="/wiki/Gritstone" title="Gritstone">gritstone</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> area of the <a href="/wiki/White_Peak" title="White Peak">White Peak</a>.
</p><p>The Peak District National Park became the first <a href="/wiki/National_parks_of_England_and_Wales" title="National parks of England and Wales">national park</a> in the United Kingdom in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> With its proximity to the cities of <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a>, and access by road and rail, it attracts millions of visitors every year.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Inhabited from the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> era, evidence exists from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Ages</a>. Settled by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Romans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, the area remained largely agricultural and mining grew in importance in the medieval era. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Arkwright" title="Richard Arkwright">Richard Arkwright</a> built his <a href="/wiki/Cotton_mill" title="Cotton mill">cotton mills</a> at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Quarrying became important as mining declined. Tourism grew after the advent of the railways, visitors attracted by the landscape, <a href="/wiki/Spa_town" title="Spa town">spa towns</a> at <a href="/wiki/Buxton" title="Buxton">Buxton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matlock_Bath" title="Matlock Bath">Matlock Bath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castleton,_Derbyshire" title="Castleton, Derbyshire">Castleton</a>'s show caves, and <a href="/wiki/Bakewell" title="Bakewell">Bakewell</a>, the national park's only town. 
</p><p>Tourism remains important for its towns and villages and their varied attractions, <a href="/wiki/Country_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Country house">country houses</a> and heritage sites. Outside the towns, walking on the extensive network of public footpaths, cycle trails, rock climbing and caving are popular pursuits.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Peak District is at the southern end of the Pennines<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and much of the area is upland above 1,000 feet (300&#160;m). Its high point is  <a href="/wiki/Kinder_Scout" title="Kinder Scout">Kinder Scout</a> at 2,087&#160;ft (636&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-Nuttall_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuttall-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Despite its name, the landscape generally lacks sharp peaks, and is characterised mostly by rounded hills, <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateaus</a>, valleys, limestone <a href="/wiki/Canyon" title="Canyon">gorges</a> and gritstone <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">escarpments</a> (the "edges").<sup id="cite_ref-Landscape_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gritstone_Edges_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gritstone_Edges-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>  The area, mostly rural, is surrounded by conurbations and large urban areas including <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield" title="Huddersfield">Huddersfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national park</a> has a formal boundary and covers most of the <a href="/wiki/Dark_Peak" title="Dark Peak">Dark Peak</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_Peak" title="White Peak">White Peak</a>, but the wider Peak District is less well defined.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Dark Peak is largely uninhabited <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorland</a> and gritstone escarpments in the northern Peak District and its eastern and western margins. It encloses the central and southern White Peak which is where most settlements, farmland and limestone gorges are located.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Character_areas_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Character_areas-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Three of <a href="/wiki/Natural_England" title="Natural England">Natural England</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Character_Area" title="National Character Area">National Character Areas</a> (NCAs) include the landscape. The Dark Peak NCA includes the northern and eastern parts of the Dark Peak while the White Peak NCA covers most of the White Peak. The western margins of the Dark Peak are in the South West Peak NCA, where farmland and pastured valleys are found as well as gritstone edges and moorland.<sup id="cite_ref-Character_areas_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Character_areas-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-South_West_Peak_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_West_Peak-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>  Outside the park, the wider Peak District is often considered to include the area approximately between <a href="/wiki/Disley" title="Disley">Disley</a> and Sterndale Moor, and some of the outer fringes and foothills, including the <a href="/wiki/River_Churnet" title="River Churnet">Churnet</a><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/River_Derwent,_Derbyshire" title="River Derwent, Derbyshire">lower Derwent</a> Valleys.<sup id="cite_ref-Slow_Travel_The_Peak_District_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slow_Travel_The_Peak_District-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The region is mostly surrounded by lowlands<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> with gritstone moorlands of the <a href="/wiki/South_Pennines" title="South Pennines">South Pennines</a> to the north.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The national park covers 555 square miles (1,440&#160;km<sup>2</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-Area_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Area-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> including most of the region in Derbyshire and extends into Staffordshire, Cheshire, Greater Manchester and South and West Yorkshire. The park's northern limits are on the <a href="/wiki/A62_road" title="A62 road">A62 road</a> between Huddersfield and <a href="/wiki/Oldham" title="Oldham">Oldham</a>, and its southernmost point is on the <a href="/wiki/A52_road" title="A52 road">A52 road</a> near <a href="/wiki/Ashbourne,_Derbyshire" title="Ashbourne, Derbyshire">Ashbourne</a>. The park boundaries were drawn to exclude built-up and industrial areas; in particular <a href="/wiki/Buxton" title="Buxton">Buxton</a> and the quarries at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Peak_Dale" title="Peak Dale">Peak Dale</a> corridor are surrounded on three sides by the park. <a href="/wiki/Bakewell" title="Bakewell">Bakewell</a> and many villages are in the national park as is much of the rural west of Sheffield. As of 2010, it is the fifth largest <a href="/wiki/National_parks_of_England_and_Wales" title="National parks of England and Wales">national park in England and Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In the UK, designation as a national park means that planning and other functions are provided by a national park authority with additional planning restrictions that provide enhanced protection against inappropriate development. Land within this national park as in other UK parks is in a mix of public and private ownership.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Trust_for_Places_of_Historic_Interest_or_Natural_Beauty" title="National Trust for Places of Historic Interest or Natural Beauty">National Trust</a>, a charity that conserves historic and natural landscapes, owns about 12% of the land in the national park.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Its three estates (<a href="/wiki/High_Peak_Estate" title="High Peak Estate">High Peak</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_Peak_Estate" title="White Peak Estate">White Peak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longshaw_Estate" title="Longshaw Estate">Longshaw</a>) include ecologically or geologically significant areas at <a href="/wiki/Bleaklow" title="Bleaklow">Bleaklow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Edge" title="Derwent Edge">Derwent Edge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hope_Woodlands" title="Hope Woodlands">Hope Woodlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinder_Scout" title="Kinder Scout">Kinder Scout</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Manifold" title="River Manifold">Manifold</a> valley, <a href="/wiki/Mam_Tor" title="Mam Tor">Mam Tor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dovedale" title="Dovedale">Dovedale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milldale_(Peak_District)" class="mw-redirect" title="Milldale (Peak District)">Milldale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winnats_Pass" title="Winnats Pass">Winnats Pass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-South_Peak_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Peak-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The park authority owns around 5%, and other major landowners include several water companies.<sup id="cite_ref-Landuse_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landuse-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg/800px-Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg" decoding="async" width="800" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg/1200px-Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg/1600px-Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="730" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Near_the_High_Peak_-_pano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/High_Peak_Estate" title="High Peak Estate">High Peak</a> panorama between <a href="/wiki/Hayfield,_Derbyshire" title="Hayfield, Derbyshire">Hayfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinley" title="Chinley">Chinley</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Settlements">Settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bakewell is the largest settlement and only town in the national park; its five-arched bridge over the River Wye dates from the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Castleton is the centre of production of the semi-precious mineral <a href="/wiki/Blue_John_(mineral)" title="Blue John (mineral)">Blue John</a> while the village of Eyam is known for its self-imposed quarantine during the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">Black Death</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edale" title="Edale">Edale</a> is the southern terminus of the <a href="/wiki/Pennine_Way" title="Pennine Way">Pennine Way</a>, a 268-mile <a href="/wiki/National_Trails" title="National Trails">national trail</a> which transverses most of the Pennines and ends at <a href="/wiki/Kirk_Yetholm" title="Kirk Yetholm">Kirk Yetholm</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Scottish_border" title="Anglo-Scottish border">Scottish border</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Other villages within the park include <a href="/wiki/Flash,_Staffordshire" title="Flash, Staffordshire">Flash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hathersage" title="Hathersage">Hathersage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hartington,_Derbyshire" title="Hartington, Derbyshire">Hartington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilam,_Staffordshire" title="Ilam, Staffordshire">Ilam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tideswell" title="Tideswell">Tideswell</a>.
</p><p>The towns of <a href="/wiki/Glossop" title="Glossop">Glossop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chapel-en-le-Frith" title="Chapel-en-le-Frith">Chapel-en-le-Frith</a>, Buxton, <a href="/wiki/Macclesfield" title="Macclesfield">Macclesfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leek,_Staffordshire" title="Leek, Staffordshire">Leek</a>, Ashbourne, <a href="/wiki/Matlock,_Derbyshire" title="Matlock, Derbyshire">Matlock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield" title="Chesterfield">Chesterfield</a> are on the fringes of the national park. The spa town of Buxton was developed by the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Devonshire" title="Duke of Devonshire">Dukes of Devonshire</a> as a genteel health resort in the 18th century<sup id="cite_ref-Visit_Buxton_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Visit_Buxton-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Spa" title="Spa">spa</a> at Matlock Bath in the valley of the River Derwent was popularised in the Victorian era. <a href="/wiki/Hayfield,_Derbyshire" title="Hayfield, Derbyshire">Hayfield</a> is at the foot of Kinder Scout, the highest summit in the area. Other towns and villages fringing the park include <a href="/wiki/Whaley_Bridge" title="Whaley Bridge">Whaley Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadfield,_Derbyshire" title="Hadfield, Derbyshire">Hadfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tintwistle" title="Tintwistle">Tintwistle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsden,_West_Yorkshire" title="Marsden, West Yorkshire">Marsden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holmfirth" title="Holmfirth">Holmfirth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stocksbridge" title="Stocksbridge">Stocksbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Darley_Dale" title="Darley Dale">Darley Dale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wirksworth" title="Wirksworth">Wirksworth</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Rivers.2C_reservoirs_and_canals"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers,_reservoirs_and_canals">Rivers, reservoirs and canals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rivers, reservoirs and canals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several rivers have their sources on the moorland plateaux of the Dark Peak and the high ridges of the White Peak. Many rivers in the Dark Peak and outer fringes were dammed to create reservoirs for supplying drinking water. Streams were dammed to provide headwater for <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">water driven mills</a>; <a href="/wiki/Weir" title="Weir">weirs</a> were built for the same purpose.
The reservoirs of the <a href="/wiki/Longdendale_Chain" title="Longdendale Chain">Longdendale Chain</a> were completed in February 1877 to provide compensation water, ensuring a continuous flow along the <a href="/wiki/River_Etherow" title="River Etherow">River Etherow</a> which was essential for local industry, and provide drinking water for Manchester.<sup id="cite_ref-Quayle_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quayle-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In a report for the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_Council" title="Manchester City Council">Manchester Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Frederick_Bateman" title="John Frederick Bateman">John Frederick Bateman</a> wrote in 1846:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Within ten or twelve miles of Manchester, and six or seven miles from the existing reservoirs at Gorton, there is this tract of mountain land abounding with springs of the purest quality. Its physical and geological features offer such peculiar features for the collection, storage and supply of water for the use of the towns in the plains below that I am surprised that they have been overlooked.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>John Frederick Bateman<sup id="cite_ref-Quayle_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quayle-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The western side of the Peak District is drained by the Etherow, <a href="/wiki/River_Goyt" title="River Goyt">Goyt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/River_Tame,_Greater_Manchester" title="River Tame, Greater Manchester">Tame</a>, which are tributaries of the <a href="/wiki/River_Mersey" title="River Mersey">River Mersey</a>. The north east is drained by tributaries of the <a href="/wiki/River_Don,_South_Yorkshire" class="mw-redirect" title="River Don, South Yorkshire">River Don</a>. Of the tributaries of the <a href="/wiki/River_Trent" title="River Trent">River Trent</a> that drain the south and east, the <a href="/wiki/River_Derwent,_Derbyshire" title="River Derwent, Derbyshire">River Derwent</a> is the most prominent. It <a href="/wiki/Source_(river_or_stream)" class="mw-redirect" title="Source (river or stream)">rises</a> on Bleaklow just east of <a href="/wiki/Glossop" title="Glossop">Glossop</a> and flows through the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Derwent_Valley" title="Upper Derwent Valley">Upper Derwent Valley</a> where it is constrained by the <a href="/wiki/Howden_Reservoir" title="Howden Reservoir">Howden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Reservoir_(Derbyshire)" title="Derwent Reservoir (Derbyshire)">Derwent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ladybower_Reservoir" title="Ladybower Reservoir">Ladybower Reservoirs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The reservoirs of the Upper Derwent Valley were built from the early to mid 20th century to supply drinking water to the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a> and South Yorkshire.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/River_Noe" title="River Noe">River Noe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/River_Wye,_Derbyshire" title="River Wye, Derbyshire">River Wye</a> are its tributaries.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/River_Manifold" title="River Manifold">River Manifold</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/River_Dove,_Central_England" title="River Dove, Central England">River Dove</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> in the south west, whose sources are on <a href="/wiki/Axe_Edge_Moor" title="Axe Edge Moor">Axe Edge Moor</a>, flow into the Trent, while the <a href="/wiki/River_Dane" title="River Dane">River Dane</a><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> flows into the <a href="/wiki/River_Weaver" title="River Weaver">River Weaver</a> in Cheshire.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bugsworth_058079.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bugsworth_058079.jpg/170px-Bugsworth_058079.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bugsworth_058079.jpg/255px-Bugsworth_058079.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bugsworth_058079.jpg/340px-Bugsworth_058079.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bugsworth_058079.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bugsworth Basin on the Peak Forest Canal.</div></div></div>
<p>There are no canals in the national park, although the <a href="/wiki/Standedge_Tunnels" title="Standedge Tunnels">Standedge Tunnels</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Narrow_Canal" title="Huddersfield Narrow Canal">Huddersfield Narrow Canal</a> run underneath the extreme north of it.  Waters from the Dark Peak feed the <a href="/wiki/Macclesfield_Canal" title="Macclesfield Canal">Macclesfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashton_Canal" title="Ashton Canal">Ashton</a>, and Huddersfield Narrow Canals and waters from the White Peak fed the <a href="/wiki/Cromford_Canal" title="Cromford Canal">Cromford Canal</a>. Outside the  park, the <a href="/wiki/Peak_Forest_Canal" title="Peak Forest Canal">Peak Forest Canal</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a> from the quarries at <a href="/wiki/Dove_Holes" title="Dove Holes">Dove Holes</a> for the construction industry. It terminated at <a href="/wiki/Bugsworth_Basin" title="Bugsworth Basin">Bugsworth Basin</a> and the journey was completed using the <a href="/wiki/Peak_Forest_Tramway" title="Peak Forest Tramway">Peak Forest Tramway</a>. 
</p><p>The Cromford Canal, from <a href="/wiki/Cromford" title="Cromford">Cromford</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Erewash_Canal" title="Erewash Canal">Erewash Canal</a>, served lead mines at <a href="/wiki/Wirksworth" title="Wirksworth">Wirksworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sir_Richard_Arkwright" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Richard Arkwright">Sir Richard Arkwright</a>'s cotton mills. The <a href="/wiki/Caldon_Canal" title="Caldon Canal">Caldon Canal</a> from <a href="/wiki/Froghall" title="Froghall">Froghall</a> was built to transport limestone from quarries at Cauldon Low for the iron industry and flints for the pottery industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Caldon_Canal_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caldon_Canal-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most of the area is more than 1,000 feet (300&#160;m) above sea level,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> in the centre of the country at a latitude of 53°N resulting in relatively high annual rainfall, averaging 40.35 inches (1,025&#160;mm) in 1999, compared with the rest of England and Wales. The Dark Peak tends to receive more rainfall then the White Peak as it is higher. The higher rainfall does not affect the temperature, which averages the same as the rest of England and Wales at 10.3&#160;°C (50.5&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>During the 1970s, the Dark Peak regularly recorded more than 70 days of snowfall. Since then the number of snowfall days has decreased. The hills still see periods of long continuous snow cover in some winters. Snow in mid-December 2009 on some hill summits created snow patches that lasted in some cases until May 2010. In the same winter, the A635 (Saddleworth Moor) and  A57 (Snake Pass) were closed because of lying snow for almost a month. Frost cover is seen for 20–30% of the winter on moorland in the Dark Peak and 10% in the White Peak.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The Moorland Indicators of Climate Change Initiative was set up in 2008 to collect data in the area. Students investigated the interaction between people and the moorlands, and their effect on climate change, to discover whether the moorlands are a net carbon sink or source, based on the fact that Britain's upland areas are a significant global carbon store in the form of <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>. Human interaction in terms of direct erosion and fire as well as the effects of global warming are the major variables that they considered.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Peak is formed almost exclusively from <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> dating from the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period. They comprise the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous_Limestone" title="Carboniferous Limestone">Carboniferous Limestone</a>, overlying <a href="/wiki/Gritstone" title="Gritstone">Gritstone</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coal_Measures" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal Measures">Coal Measures</a> which occur only on the extreme margins and infrequent outcrops of <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a> including <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lavas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuff" title="Tuff">tuffs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic vent</a> <a href="/wiki/Agglomerate" title="Agglomerate">agglomerates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The general <a href="/wiki/Structural_geology" title="Structural geology">geological structure</a> is that of a broad <a href="/wiki/Dome_(geology)" title="Dome (geology)">dome</a>, whose western margins have been most intensely <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faulted</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folded</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Geologic_uplift" class="mw-redirect" title="Geologic uplift">Uplift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> have effectively sliced the top off the dome to reveal a <a href="/wiki/Concentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentric">concentric</a> <a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">outcrop</a> pattern with Coal Measures rocks on the eastern and western margins, Carboniferous Limestone at the core and with rocks of <a href="/wiki/Millstone_Grit" title="Millstone Grit">Millstone Grit</a> outcropping between them. The southern edge of the dome is overlain by <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a> of <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> age though they barely impinge on the National Park. The White Peak is the central and southern section  where Carboniferous Limestone is found at or near the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-NCA_Profile:_52_White_Peak_(NE534)_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCA_Profile:_52_White_Peak_(NE534)-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Dark Peak to the north, east and west is characterised by Millstone Grit outcrops and broad swathes of moorland.<sup id="cite_ref-NCA_Profile:_51_Dark_Peak_(NE378)_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCA_Profile:_51_Dark_Peak_(NE378)-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-South_West_Peak_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_West_Peak-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Earth movements after the Carboniferous period resulted in the up-doming of the area and, particularly in the west, the folding of the rock strata along north–south axes. The region was raised in a north–south line which resulted in the dome-like shape<sup id="cite_ref-Geology_explained_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geology_explained-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shales</a> and sandstones were worn away until limestone was exposed. At the end of this period, the Earth's crust sank here which led to the area being covered by sea, depositing a variety of new rocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Geology_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geology-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Some time after its deposition, mineral veins were formed in the limestone. The veins and rakes have been mined for lead since <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman times</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Geology_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geology-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thor%27s_cave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Thor%27s_cave.jpg/170px-Thor%27s_cave.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Thor%27s_cave.jpg/255px-Thor%27s_cave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Thor%27s_cave.jpg/340px-Thor%27s_cave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="493" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thor%27s_cave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thor%27s_Cave" title="Thor&#39;s Cave">Thor's Cave</a> seen from the <a href="/wiki/Manifold_Way" title="Manifold Way">Manifold Way</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Peak District was covered by ice during at least one of the <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a> of the last 2&#160;million years (probably the <a href="/wiki/Anglian_(stage)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglian (stage)">Anglian</a> glaciation of around 450,000 years ago) as evidenced by the patches of glacial till or boulder clay that can be found across the area.  It was not covered by ice during the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>, which peaked around 20,000–22,000 years ago. A mix of Irish Sea and Lake District ice abutted against its western margins. Glacial meltwaters eroded a complex of sinuous channels along this margin of the district during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Glacial meltwaters contributed to the formation and development of many caves in the limestone area.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Wild animal herds roamed the area, and their remains have been found in several of the local caves.<sup id="cite_ref-Geology_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geology-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The different types of rock beneath the soil strongly influence the landscape; they determine the type of <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a> that will grow, and ultimately the type of animal that will inhabit the area.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">Limestone</a> has fissures and is <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> in water, therefore rivers have been able to carve deep, narrow valleys. The rivers then often find routes underground, creating cave systems. Millstone Grit is insoluble but <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porous</a>, absorbing water which seeps through the grits, until it meets the less porous shales beneath, creating <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a> where it reaches the surface. The shales are <a href="/wiki/Friability" title="Friability">friable</a> and easily attacked by <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">frost</a> and form areas that are vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, as on Mam Tor.<sup id="cite_ref-Geology_explained_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geology_explained-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The gritstone and shale of the Dark Peak supports <a href="/wiki/Ericaceae" title="Ericaceae">heather</a> moorland and <a href="/wiki/Blanket_bog" title="Blanket bog">blanket bog</a> environments, with rough <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">sheep</a> pasture and <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a> shooting being the main land uses, although parts of it also support farming,<sup id="cite_ref-NCA_Profile:_51_Dark_Peak_(NE378)_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCA_Profile:_51_Dark_Peak_(NE378)-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> especially the South West Peak NCA.<sup id="cite_ref-NCA_Profile:_53_South_West_Peak_(NE453)_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCA_Profile:_53_South_West_Peak_(NE453)-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The limestone plateaux of the White Peak are more intensively farmed, with mainly dairy usage of improved pastures.<sup id="cite_ref-NCA_Profile:_52_White_Peak_(NE534)_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCA_Profile:_52_White_Peak_(NE534)-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>  Woodland forms around 8% of the Peak National Park.<sup id="cite_ref-Landuse_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landuse-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Natural broad-leaved <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a> is found in the steep-sided, narrow dales of the <a href="/wiki/White_Peak" title="White Peak">White Peak</a> and the deep cloughs of the Dark Peak, while reservoir margins often have coniferous plantations. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polemonium_caeruleum" title="Polemonium caeruleum">Jacob's-ladder</a> (<i>Polemonium caeruleum</i>), a relatively rare species characteristic of limestone dales in the White Peak, has been the county flower of Derbyshire since 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire._48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire.-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> It grows in grassland, light woodland, on screes, rock ledges and by streams in <a href="/wiki/River_Lathkill" title="River Lathkill">Lathkill</a>, Wolfscote, Taddington, <a href="/wiki/River_Wye,_Derbyshire" title="River Wye, Derbyshire">Wye</a> Dale and other dales.  Pollen evidence from peat bogs indicates that it was widespread throughout Britain just after the last ice-age. Much planted in gardens from where it has established itself in other parts of the area, as a native it is restricted to the White Peak and the <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Dales" title="Yorkshire Dales">Yorkshire Dales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Three <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> vascular plants are found nowhere else in the world: <i><a href="/wiki/Rubus_durescens" title="Rubus durescens">Rubus durescens</a></i> is a bramble found in central Derbyshire;<sup id="cite_ref-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire._48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire.-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Derby hawkweed (<i><a href="/wiki/Hieracium_naviense" title="Hieracium naviense">Hieracium naviense</a></i>), found only in <a href="/wiki/Winnats_Pass" title="Winnats Pass">Winnats Pass</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire._48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire.-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> is a native perennial of limestone cliffs that was discovered by J.N. Mills in 1966 and described as a new species in 1968;<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and leek-coloured hawkweed (<i>H. subprasinifolium</i>) was believed extinct but was rediscovered on banks beside the <a href="/wiki/Monsal_Trail" title="Monsal Trail">Monsal Trail</a> in Cheedale in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  The endemic Derbyshire feather moss <i><a href="/wiki/Thamnobryum_angustifolium" title="Thamnobryum angustifolium">Thamnobryum angustifolium</a></i> occurs in one Derbyshire limestone dale, its sole world location intentionally kept confidential; the colony covers about 3 square metres (32&#160;sq&#160;ft) of a rock face with small subsidiary colonies nearby.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Dark Peak's heathlands, bogs, gritstone edges and acid grasslands contain relatively few species and <a href="/wiki/Calluna" title="Calluna">heather</a> (<i>Calluna vulgaris</i>), <a href="/wiki/Empetrum_nigrum" title="Empetrum nigrum">crowberry</a> (<i>Empetrum nigrum</i>), <a href="/wiki/Vaccinium_myrtillus" title="Vaccinium myrtillus">bilberry</a> (<i>Vaccinium myrtillus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eriophorum_vaginatum" title="Eriophorum vaginatum">hare's-tail cotton grass</a> (<i>Eriophorum vaginatum</i>) are dominant on the high moors.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire._48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire.-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  After decades of decline due to pollution, <i>Sphagnum</i> mosses are making a comeback, with species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sphagnum_cuspidatum" title="Sphagnum cuspidatum">S. cuspidatum</a></i> being particularly dominant.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> White Peak habitats include <a href="/wiki/Calcareous_grassland" title="Calcareous grassland">calcareous grassland</a>, ash woodlands and rock outcrops suitable for lime-loving species.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire._48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Flora_of_Derbyshire.-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> They include <a href="/wiki/Orchis_mascula" title="Orchis mascula">early purple orchid</a> (<i>Orchis mascula</i>), <a href="/wiki/Epipactis_atrorubens" title="Epipactis atrorubens">dark-red helleborine</a> (<i>Epipactis atrorubens</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ophrys_insectifera" title="Ophrys insectifera">fly orchid</a> (<i>Ophrys insectifera</i>), <a href="/wiki/Helianthemum_nummularium" title="Helianthemum nummularium">common rockrose</a> (<i>Helianthemum nummularium</i>), <a href="/wiki/Helianthemum_nummularium" title="Helianthemum nummularium">spring cinquefoil</a> (<i>Helianthemum nummularium</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Parnassia" title="Parnassia">grass of parnassus</a> (<i>Parnassia palustris</i>). Lead rakes, the spoil heaps of ancient mining activity, form another distinctive habitat in the White Peak, supporting a range of rare <a href="/wiki/Metallophyte" title="Metallophyte">metallophyte</a> plants, including the <a href="/wiki/Minuartia" title="Minuartia">spring sandwort</a> (<i>Minuartia verna</i>; also known as leadwort), <a href="/wiki/Thlaspi_caerulescens" title="Thlaspi caerulescens">alpine pennycress</a> (<i>Thlaspi caerulescens</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Viola_lutea" title="Viola lutea">mountain pansy</a> (<i>Viola lutea</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lead_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lead-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most mammals found in the Peak District are generalists and widespread across the UK, but <a href="/wiki/Mountain_hare" title="Mountain hare">mountain hares</a> on heather moorland in the Dark Peak constitute the only wild population in England. They were re-introduced in the Victorian era for sporting purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  A feral population of <a href="/wiki/Red-necked_wallabies" class="mw-redirect" title="Red-necked wallabies">red-necked wallabies</a> lived around <a href="/wiki/The_Roaches" title="The Roaches">The Roaches</a> from the 1940s onwards but may now be extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Red_deer" title="Red deer">Red deer</a> herds, assumed to be derived from animals escaped from <a href="/wiki/Deer_park_(England)" title="Deer park (England)">deer parks</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lyme_Park" title="Lyme Park">Lyme Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chatsworth_House" title="Chatsworth House">Chatsworth</a>, are established in the upper reaches of the <a href="/wiki/River_Goyt" title="River Goyt">Goyt valley</a> and on the moors above <a href="/wiki/Baslow" title="Baslow">Baslow</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and a herd on <a href="/wiki/Wharncliffe_Crags" title="Wharncliffe Crags">Wharncliffe Crags</a> outside the park north of Sheffield may derive from the hunting stock of Wharncliffe Chase.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_action_plan" title="Biodiversity action plan">Biodiversity action plans</a> have been prepared for mountain hare, <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">brown hare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_long-eared_bat" title="Brown long-eared bat">brown long-eared bat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hazel_dormouse" title="Hazel dormouse">dormouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_harvest_mouse" title="Eurasian harvest mouse">harvest mouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_hedgehog" title="European hedgehog">hedgehog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noctule_bat" class="mw-redirect" title="Noctule bat">noctule bat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_otter" title="Eurasian otter">otter</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_pine_marten" title="European pine marten">pine marten</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_polecat" title="European polecat">polecat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soprano_pipistrelle" title="Soprano pipistrelle">soprano pipistrelle</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_water_vole" title="European water vole">water vole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The status of the pine marten is unclear, although confirmed sightings have occurred in recent decades in Derbyshire and north Staffordshire<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and a specimen from an introduced Welsh population<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> was found dead outside the national park on a road between <a href="/wiki/Ripley,_Derbyshire" title="Ripley, Derbyshire">Ripley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belper" title="Belper">Belper</a> in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many of the Peak's bird species are also widespread generalists. The moors of the Dark Peak support breeding populations of a number of upland specialists, such as <a href="/wiki/Twite" title="Twite">twite</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pdnp_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdnp-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pdnp_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdnp-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_golden_plover" title="European golden plover">golden plover</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pdnp_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdnp-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dunlin" title="Dunlin">dunlin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ring_ouzel" title="Ring ouzel">ring ouzel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pdnp_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdnp-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northern_wheatear" title="Northern wheatear">northern wheatear</a><sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Birds_Project_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Birds_Project-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pdnp_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdnp-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The populations of twite and golden plover are the southernmost confirmed breeding populations in England,<sup id="cite_ref-bto_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and the Peak District Moors Special Protection Area (SPA) is a European designation for its populations of merlin, golden plover and short-eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-pdnp_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdnp-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Peak District lacks the concentrations of breeding <a href="/wiki/Wader" title="Wader">waders</a> found further north in the Pennines, although the moors and their fringes accommodate breeding <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_curlew" title="Eurasian curlew">curlew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_lapwing" title="Northern lapwing">lapwing</a><sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Birds_Project_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Birds_Project-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> as well as less noticeable wading birds including dunlin and <a href="/wiki/Common_snipe" title="Common snipe">snipe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Birds_Project_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Birds_Project-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial <a href="/wiki/Driven_grouse_shooting" title="Driven grouse shooting">driven grouse shooting</a> occurs on the heather moorlands of the Dark Peak, where the <a href="/wiki/Red_grouse" title="Red grouse">red grouse</a> population is maintained by gamekeepers employed by shooting estates.<sup id="cite_ref-marsland_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marsland-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-malp_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malp-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> A population of <a href="/wiki/Black_grouse" title="Black grouse">black grouse</a> became extinct in 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-bto_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> but reintroduction was attempted in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Quarries and rock outcrops provide nest sites for <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a>. Ravens and <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> are increasingly encountered as their British range expands eastwards, perhaps because of general reductions in persecution. Illegal persecution may be implicated in the limited populations of rare raptors such as <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a>, peregrine and <a href="/wiki/Hen_harrier" title="Hen harrier">hen harrier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-malp_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malp-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/RSPB" class="mw-redirect" title="RSPB">RSPB</a>'s publication of <i>Peak Malpractice</i>, a 2006 report highlighting wildlife crime,<sup id="cite_ref-malp_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malp-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> the Peak District Bird of Prey Initiative was set up in 2011 by conservationists and shooting bodies in an attempt to boost the populations of birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The park authorities expressed disappointment at the limited results<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and the RSPB withdrew from the partnership in January 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fast-flowing rivers attract specialist species, notably <a href="/wiki/Grey_wagtail" title="Grey wagtail">grey wagtail</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-marsland_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marsland-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/White-throated_dipper" title="White-throated dipper">dipper</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-lgre_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lgre-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Common_sandpiper" title="Common sandpiper">common sandpiper</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-marsland_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marsland-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_duck" title="Mandarin duck">mandarin duck</a><sup id="cite_ref-birdreport_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdreport-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Goosander" class="mw-redirect" title="Goosander">goosander</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>  Wooded and semi-wooded areas attract <a href="/wiki/Common_redstart" title="Common redstart">redstart</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Birds_Project_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Birds_Project-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_pied_flycatcher" title="European pied flycatcher">pied flycatcher</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Birds_Project_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Birds_Project-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wood_warbler" title="Wood warbler">wood warbler</a><sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Birds_Project_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Birds_Project-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tree_pipit" title="Tree pipit">tree pipit</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-marsland_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marsland-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and coniferous plantations house <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_siskin" title="Eurasian siskin">siskin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_crossbill" class="mw-redirect" title="Common crossbill">common crossbill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lgre_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lgre-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Upland reservoirs in the Dark Peak are generally <a href="/wiki/Oligotroph" title="Oligotroph">oligotrophic</a> and attract few birds, but lower-lying reservoirs on the southern fringes such as <a href="/wiki/Carsington_Water" title="Carsington Water">Carsington Water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ogston_Reservoir" title="Ogston Reservoir">Ogston Reservoir</a> regularly attract rare migrants and wintering rarities such as various waders, wildfowl, gulls and terns.<sup id="cite_ref-birdreport_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdreport-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The area is regularly overflown by wintering populations of <a href="/wiki/Pink-footed_geese" class="mw-redirect" title="Pink-footed geese">pink-footed geese</a><sup id="cite_ref-birdreport_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdreport-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> moving between East Anglia and Morecambe Bay.
</p><p>Dipper, golden plover, hen harrier, merlin and short-eared owl are local <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_action_plan" title="Biodiversity action plan">biodiversity action plan</a> priority species.<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_taxa">Other taxa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other taxa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Amphibians and reptiles such as <a href="/wiki/Common_lizard" class="mw-redirect" title="Common lizard">common lizards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grass_snake" title="Grass snake">grass snakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_crested_newt" class="mw-redirect" title="Great crested newt">great crested newts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slow_worm" class="mw-redirect" title="Slow worm">slow worms</a> are found in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The eastern moors are a stronghold for <a href="/wiki/Vipera_berus" title="Vipera berus">adders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Native fish in the Peak District include <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_salmon" title="Atlantic salmon">Atlantic salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_trout" title="Brown trout">brown trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_eel" title="European eel">European eel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_bullhead" title="European bullhead">bullhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brook_lamprey" title="Brook lamprey">brook lamprey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thymallus" title="Thymallus">grayling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> A possibly unique population of "wild" <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">rainbow trout</a> survives on the <a href="/wiki/River_Wye,_Derbyshire" title="River Wye, Derbyshire">Derbyshire Wye</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> following their introduction at the turn of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Butterflies in the region include the <a href="/wiki/Dingy_skipper" title="Dingy skipper">dingy skipper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_argus" class="mw-redirect" title="Brown argus">brown argus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Small_blue" title="Small blue">small blue</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-letter_hairstreak" title="White-letter hairstreak">white-letter hairstreak</a>. Moths include the <a href="/wiki/Stilbia_anomala" title="Stilbia anomala">anomalous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceramica_pisi" title="Ceramica pisi">broom moth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dot_moth" title="Dot moth">dot moth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garden_dart" title="Garden dart">garden dart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouse_moth" title="Mouse moth">mouse moth</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_ermine" class="mw-redirect" title="White ermine">white ermine</a>. Other invertebrates include the <a href="/wiki/Bilberry_bumblebee" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilberry bumblebee">bilberry bumblebee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monocephalus" title="Monocephalus">broad groove-head spider</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mole_cricket" title="Mole cricket">mole cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipsothrix" title="Lipsothrix">northern yellow splinter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shining_guest_ant" class="mw-redirect" title="Shining guest ant">shining guest ant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meloe_violaceus" title="Meloe violaceus">violet oil beetle</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-clawed_crayfish" class="mw-redirect" title="White-clawed crayfish">white-clawed crayfish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity_Action_Plan_Species_Found_in_the_Peak_District-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kinder_plateau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kinder_plateau.jpg/170px-Kinder_plateau.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kinder_plateau.jpg/255px-Kinder_plateau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kinder_plateau.jpg/340px-Kinder_plateau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2469" data-file-height="3306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kinder_plateau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view of the <a href="/wiki/Edale" title="Edale">Edale</a> valley from <a href="/wiki/Mam_Tor" title="Mam Tor">Mam Tor</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Peak has been inhabited from the earliest periods of human activity as evidenced by finds of <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> flint artefacts and <a href="/wiki/Palaeogeography" title="Palaeogeography">palaeoenvironmental</a> evidence from caves in Dovedale and elsewhere. Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> activity includes monumental earthworks or <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">barrows</a> such as that at <a href="/wiki/Margery_Hill" title="Margery Hill">Margery Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> the area was well populated and farmed, and evidence of these people survives in <a href="/wiki/Henge" title="Henge">henges</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Arbor_Low" title="Arbor Low">Arbor Low</a> near <a href="/wiki/Youlgreave" title="Youlgreave">Youlgreave</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Ladies" title="Nine Ladies">Nine Ladies stone circle</a> at <a href="/wiki/Stanton_Moor" title="Stanton Moor">Stanton Moor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In the same period, and into the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> hillforts such as that at Mam Tor were created. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Romans</a> exploited the area's rich mineral veins, exporting lead from the Buxton area along well-used routes. Buxton was a Roman settlement, known as "Aquae Arnemetiae" in recognition of its spring.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> .
</p><p>Theories as to the derivation of the Peak name include that it came from the <a href="/wiki/Pecsaetan" title="Pecsaetan">Pecsaetan</a> or peaklanders, an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> tribe who inhabited the central and northern parts of the area from the 6th century AD when it was part of the Anglian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Barrows from the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> period are present, including at <a href="/wiki/Benty_Grange" title="Benty Grange">Benty Grange</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Benty_Grange_helmet" title="Benty Grange helmet">eponymous helmet</a> was found.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining_and_quarrying">Mining and quarrying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mining and quarrying">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire_lead_mining_history" title="Derbyshire lead mining history">Derbyshire lead mining history</a></div>
<p>In medieval and early modern times the area was mainly agricultural with sheep farming, rather than arable, the main activity in upland holdings. From the 16th century, the area's mineral and geological wealth became increasingly significant. Not only lead, but coal, <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a>, copper from <a href="/wiki/Ecton_Mines" title="Ecton Mines">Ecton Mines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> and silver have all been mined.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Celia_Fiennes" title="Celia Fiennes">Celia Fiennes</a>, describing her journey through the Peak in 1697, described,
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...&#160;craggy hills whose bowells are full of mines of all kinds off black and white and veined marbles, and some have mines of copper, others tinn and leaden mines, in w[hi]ch is a great deale of silver.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Celia_Fiennes" title="Celia Fiennes">Celia Fiennes</a><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Coal measures occur on the Peak's western and eastern fringes and evidence of past workings can be found from Glossop to <a href="/wiki/The_Roaches" title="The Roaches">The Roaches</a>, and from <a href="/wiki/Stocksbridge" title="Stocksbridge">Stocksbridge</a> to <a href="/wiki/Baslow" title="Baslow">Baslow</a>. The coal measures in the east are at the western edge of the <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire_Coalfield" title="South Yorkshire Coalfield">South Yorkshire Coalfield</a><sup id="cite_ref-The_Yorkshire_Coalfield_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Yorkshire_Coalfield-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and those in the west are part of the Cheshire section of the <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_Coalfield" title="Lancashire Coalfield">Lancashire Coalfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Manchester_and_Its_Region_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manchester_and_Its_Region-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Mining started in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval times</a> and was at its most productive in the 18th and early 19th centuries and continued into the early 20th century. The earliest mining took place at and close to <a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">outcrops</a> where miners followed the seams deeper into the hillsides. At Goyt's Moss and <a href="/wiki/Axe_Edge_Moor" title="Axe Edge Moor">Axe Edge</a>, deep seams were worked and steam engines raised the coal and dewatered the mines.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Coal from the east was used in lead smelting and from the west for lime burning.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lead mining peaked in the 17th and 18th centuries; high concentrations were found in the area from this period, as well as discovering peat on Kinder Scout suggesting that lead smelting occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-Mining_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mining-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Lead mining declined from the mid-19th century and the last major mine closed in 1939. Lead is a by-product of fluorite, baryte and calcite mining.<sup id="cite_ref-Lead_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lead-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bell_pit" title="Bell pit">Bell pits</a> were sunk to access ore that lay close to the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-Mining_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mining-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fluorite or fluorspar is called <a href="/wiki/Blue_John_(mineral)" title="Blue John (mineral)">Blue John</a> locally, its name possibly from the French <i>bleu et jaune</i> describing its colour. Blue John is scarce, and now only a few hundred kilograms are mined each year for ornamental and <a href="/wiki/Lapidary" title="Lapidary">lapidary</a> use. The <a href="/wiki/Blue_John_Cavern" title="Blue John Cavern">Blue John Cavern</a> in Castleton is a <a href="/wiki/Show_cave" title="Show cave">show cave</a>. Small scale mining takes place in <a href="/wiki/Treak_Cliff_Cavern" title="Treak Cliff Cavern">Treak Cliff Cavern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Industrial limestone quarrying to make soda ash started around Buxton in 1874. In 1926 the operation became part of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">ICI</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Large-scale limestone and gritstone quarrying flourished as lead mining declined, and is an important if contentious industry.  Of the twelve large limestone quarries in operation, <a href="/wiki/Tunstead,_Derbyshire" title="Tunstead, Derbyshire">Tunstead</a> is one of the largest in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Total limestone output was substantial: at the 1990 peak, 8.5 million tonnes was produced.<sup id="cite_ref-Mineral_factsheet_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mineral_factsheet-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textiles">Textiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Textiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Textiles have been exported for hundreds of years. In the 14th century, the area traded in unprocessed wool.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> There was a number of skilled hand spinners and weavers in the area. By the 1780s, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Arkwright" title="Richard Arkwright">Richard Arkwright</a> had developed machinery to produce textiles more quickly and to a higher standard. The early Arkwright <a href="/wiki/Cotton_mill" title="Cotton mill">mills</a> were of light construction, narrow, about 9 feet (2.7&#160;m) wide, and low, the ceiling height was only 6 to 8 feet (1.8 to 2.4&#160;m) and were lit by daylight. The new machines were powered by <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The Peak was the ideal location, with its rivers and humid atmosphere. The local pool of labour was quickly exhausted and <a href="/wiki/Litton_Mill" title="Litton Mill">Litton Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cressbrook" title="Cressbrook">Cressbrook Mill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Millers_Dale" title="Millers Dale">Millers Dale</a> brought in children as young as four from the <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouses</a> of London as apprentices.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As technology advanced, narrow valleys proved unsuitable for larger steam-driven mills, but the Derbyshire mills remained, trading in finishing and niche products. Glossop benefitted from the textile industry. Its economy was linked closely with a spinning and weaving tradition that had evolved from developments in <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacture_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution">textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution</a>. Until the First World War, Glossop had the headquarters of the largest <a href="/wiki/Calico_(textile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Calico (textile)">textile</a> printworks in the world but after the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_crash" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street crash">Wall Street crash</a>, its product lines became vulnerable and the industry declined.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mass_trespass_of_Kinder_Scout" title="Mass trespass of Kinder Scout">Kinder Trespass</a> in 1932 was a landmark in the campaign for open access to moorland in Britain and eventually led to the formation of Britain's <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>. Before the trespass, open moorland was closed to all. Moorland estates were the private property of landed gentry who used them for only 12 days a year and were guarded by their gamekeepers.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The Peak District National Park became the United Kingdom's first national park on 17 April 1951. Campaigner <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Haythornthwaite" title="Ethel Haythornthwaite">Ethel Haythornthwaite</a> played an important part in the development of the national park. The first <a href="/wiki/Long-distance_footpaths_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Long-distance footpaths in the United Kingdom">long-distance footpath</a> in the United Kingdom was the <a href="/wiki/Pennine_Way" title="Pennine Way">Pennine Way</a>, which opened in 1965 and starts at the Nags Head Inn, in Grindsbook Booth, part of <a href="/wiki/Edale" title="Edale">Edale</a> village.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Saddleworth_Moor" title="Saddleworth Moor">Saddleworth Moor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wessenden_Valley" title="Wessenden Valley">Wessenden</a>, above Meltham, gained notoriety after the <a href="/wiki/Moors_murders" title="Moors murders">Moors murders</a> in the 1960s.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg/220px-Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg/330px-Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg/440px-Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tunstead_Quarry_-_geograph.org.uk_-_865006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tunsted Quarry</div></div></div>
<p>Tourism is estimated to provide 500 full-time jobs, 350 part-time jobs and 100 seasonal jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-Waugh_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
It is a major source of employment for park residents (24%). Manufacturing industries (19%), <a href="/wiki/Peak_District#Quarrying" title="Peak District">quarrying</a> (12%) and agriculture (12%) are also important.<sup id="cite_ref-Place_called_home_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Place_called_home-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> 
The <a href="/wiki/Hope_Construction_Materials" class="mw-redirect" title="Hope Construction Materials">cement works</a> at <a href="/wiki/Hope_Valley,_Derbyshire" title="Hope Valley, Derbyshire">Hope</a> is the largest single employer in the park.<sup id="cite_ref-Mineral_factsheet_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mineral_factsheet-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Limestone is the most important mineral quarried, mainly for roads and cement; shale is extracted for cement at Hope, and several <a href="/wiki/Gritstone" title="Gritstone">gritstone</a> quarries are worked for building stone.<sup id="cite_ref-Mineral_factsheet_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mineral_factsheet-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire_lead_mining_history" title="Derbyshire lead mining history">Lead mining</a> is no longer economic, but <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baryte" title="Baryte">baryte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> are extracted from lead veins, and small-scale Blue John mining occurs at Castleton.
</p><p>
The springs at Buxton and Ashbourne are exploited to produce bottled <a href="/wiki/Mineral_water" title="Mineral water">mineral water</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> are managed for <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">timber</a>. Other manufacturing industries include <a href="/wiki/David_Mellor_(cutler)" class="mw-redirect" title="David Mellor (cutler)">David Mellor</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cutlery" title="Cutlery">cutlery</a> factory in <a href="/wiki/Hathersage" title="Hathersage">Hathersage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferodo" title="Ferodo">Ferodo</a> <a href="/wiki/Brake_lining" title="Brake lining">brake linings</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chapel-en-le-Frith" title="Chapel-en-le-Frith">Chapel-en-le-Frith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic equipment</a> in Castleton. Of the approximately 2,700 farms in the national park, most covering less than 40 hectares (99 acres), 60% of farms are run on a part-time basis where the farmer has a second job.<sup id="cite_ref-Waugh_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> </p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>De Mirabilibus Pecci</i> or <i>The Seven Wonders of the Peak</i> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> was an early tourist description published in 1636.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Much scorn was poured on the seven wonders by visitors, including <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a> who described the moors by <a href="/wiki/Chatsworth,_Derbyshire" title="Chatsworth, Derbyshire">Chatsworth</a> as "a waste and houling wilderness" and was particularly contemptuous of <a href="/wiki/Peak_Cavern" title="Peak Cavern">Peak Cavern</a> near Castleton which was known as the 'Devil's Arse'.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Visitor numbers did not increase until the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> when the advent of railways provided access and a cultural appreciation of the <a href="/wiki/Picturesque" title="Picturesque">Picturesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> developed. <a href="/wiki/John_Mawe" title="John Mawe">John Mawe</a>'s <i>Mineralogy of Derbyshire</i> (1802)<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> and William Adam's <i>Gem of the Peak</i> (1843)<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> generated interest in the area's unique geology.
</p><p>A tradition of public access and outdoor recreation grew in the area which is  natural hinterland and rural escape for the populations of industrial Manchester and Sheffield, and remains a valuable leisure resource in a largely <a href="/wiki/Post-industrial_economy" title="Post-industrial economy">post-industrial economy</a>. In a 2005 survey of visitors, 85% of respondents mentioned "scenery and landscape" as a reason for visiting.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
