<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buxton">Buxton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Buxton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buxtoncres1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Buxtoncres1.jpg/220px-Buxtoncres1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Buxtoncres1.jpg/330px-Buxtoncres1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Buxtoncres1.jpg/440px-Buxtoncres1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buxtoncres1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buxton Crescent and St John's well</div></div></div>
<p>Buxton developed as a spa town because of its geothermal spring which rises at a constant temperature of 28&#160;°C. It was settled by the Romans around AD 78, when it was known as Aquae Arnemetiae, or the spa of the goddess of the grove. <a href="/wiki/Bess_of_Hardwick" title="Bess of Hardwick">Bess of Hardwick</a> and her husband the <a href="/wiki/George_Talbot,_6th_Earl_of_Shrewsbury" title="George Talbot, 6th Earl of Shrewsbury">Earl of Shrewsbury</a>, "took the waters" in 1569, and brought <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, there in 1573.<sup id="cite_ref-Buxton06_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buxton06-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The town largely grew in importance in the late 18th century when it was developed by the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Devonshire" title="Duke of Devonshire">5th Duke of Devonshire</a> in style of the spa of <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>.
</p><p>A resurgence in the Victorian era attracted <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin" title="Erasmus Darwin">Dr Erasmus Darwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood">Josiah Wedgwood</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> who were drawn by the reputed healing properties of the waters. The railway reached Buxton in 1863.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
Buxton's notable buildings are The Crescent (1780–1784), modelled on Bath's <a href="/wiki/Royal_Crescent" title="Royal Crescent">Royal Crescent</a>, by <a href="/wiki/John_Carr_(architect)" title="John Carr (architect)">John Carr</a>, The Devonshire (1780–1789), The Natural Baths, and The Pump Room by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Currey_(architect)" title="Henry Currey (architect)">Henry Currey</a>. The Pavilion Gardens were opened in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-Buxton06_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buxton06-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buxton_Opera_House" title="Buxton Opera House">Buxton Opera House</a> was designed by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Matcham" title="Frank Matcham">Frank Matcham</a> in 1903.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_buildings">Historic buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Historic buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Historic buildings in the park include <a href="/wiki/Chatsworth_House" title="Chatsworth House">Chatsworth House</a>, seat of the Dukes of Devonshire and among Britain's finest stately homes; the medieval <a href="/wiki/Haddon_Hall" title="Haddon Hall">Haddon Hall</a>, seat of the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Rutland" title="Duke of Rutland">Dukes of Rutland</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Lyme_Park" title="Lyme Park">Lyme Park</a>, an Elizabethan manor house transformed by an <a href="/wiki/Italianate_architecture" title="Italianate architecture">Italianate</a> front. Other historic buildings in the park include <a href="/wiki/Eyam_Hall" title="Eyam Hall">Eyam Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilam_Park" title="Ilam Park">Ilam Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tissington_Hall" title="Tissington Hall">Tissington Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Many villages and towns have fine parish churches, including the 14th-century <a href="/wiki/St_John_the_Baptist,_Tideswell" title="St John the Baptist, Tideswell">Church of St John the Baptist</a> at Tideswell, sometimes dubbed the 'Cathedral of the Peak', and the 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St_Nicholas,_Bradfield" title="Church of St Nicholas, Bradfield">Church of St Nicholas</a> at <a href="/wiki/High_Bradfield" title="High Bradfield">High Bradfield</a>. '<a href="/wiki/Little_John" title="Little John">Little John</a>'s Grave' is in the churchyard at <a href="/wiki/Hathersage" title="Hathersage">Hathersage</a>. <a href="/wiki/Peveril_Castle" title="Peveril Castle">Peveril Castle</a>, overlooking Castleton, was built by the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_District_Attractions_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_District_Attractions-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_and_attractions">Museums and attractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Museums and attractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Inside the park, <a href="/wiki/Eyam_Museum" title="Eyam Museum">Eyam Museum</a> has displays about the village's history during the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">Black Death</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eyam_Museum_and_Plague_Village_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eyam_Museum_and_Plague_Village-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Castleton has four show caves; <a href="/wiki/Peak_Cavern" title="Peak Cavern">Peak Cavern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_John_Cavern" title="Blue John Cavern">Blue John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treak_Cliff_Cavern" title="Treak Cliff Cavern">Treak Cliff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speedwell_Cavern" title="Speedwell Cavern">Speedwell</a>. In the outer fringes, the area's industrial heritage is represented by the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District_Mining_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Peak District Mining Museum">mining museum</a> at Matlock Bath with the Temple Lead Mine, the <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Valley_Mills" title="Derwent Valley Mills">Derwent Valley Mills</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brindley_Water_Mill" title="Brindley Water Mill">Brindley Water Mill</a> at Leek. Preserved railways such as the <a href="/wiki/Peak_Rail" title="Peak Rail">Peak Rail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesbourne_Valley_Railway" title="Ecclesbourne Valley Railway">Ecclesbourne Valley</a><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Churnet_Valley_Railway" title="Churnet Valley Railway">Churnet Valley</a><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> lines, the <a href="/wiki/National_Tramway_Museum" title="National Tramway Museum">National Tramway Museum</a> at <a href="/wiki/Crich" title="Crich">Crich</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cromford_Canal" title="Cromford Canal">Cromford Canal</a> chart the area's transport history. Matlock Bath also has the show caves and mines of the <a href="/wiki/Heights_of_Abraham" title="Heights of Abraham">Heights of Abraham</a> which can be reached by cable car, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Kingdom" title="Gulliver&#39;s Kingdom">Gulliver's Kingdom</a> theme park.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_District_Attractions_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_District_Attractions-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Buxton has an opera house and theatre, museum and art gallery and <a href="/wiki/Poole%27s_Cavern" title="Poole&#39;s Cavern">Poole's Cavern</a> show cave.<sup id="cite_ref-Visit_Buxton_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Visit_Buxton-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Other attractions in the fringes include the <a href="/wiki/Alton_Towers" title="Alton Towers">Alton Towers</a> theme park<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_District_Attractions_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_District_Attractions-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Peak_Wildlife_Park" title="Peak Wildlife Park">Peak Wildlife Park</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg/200px-Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg/300px-Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg 2x" data-file-width="385" data-file-height="462" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Welldressing_Hayfield.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Well_dressing" title="Well dressing">well dressing</a> at Hayfield.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Well_dressing" title="Well dressing">Well dressing</a> ceremonies are held in many villages during the spring and summer months, a tradition said to date from pagan times.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Other local customs include <a href="/wiki/Castleton_Garland_Day" title="Castleton Garland Day">Castleton Garland Day</a> and Ashbourne's <a href="/wiki/Royal_Shrovetide_Football" title="Royal Shrovetide Football">Royal Shrovetide Football</a>, played annually since the 12th century. Buxton hosts two opera festivals, the <a href="/wiki/Buxton_Festival" title="Buxton Festival">Buxton Festival</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Gilbert_and_Sullivan_Festival" title="International Gilbert and Sullivan Festival">International Gilbert and Sullivan Festival</a>, and the Buxton Festival Fringe, and the <a href="/wiki/Peak_Literary_Festival" title="Peak Literary Festival">Peak Literary Festival</a> is held at various locations twice a year. Peak District food specialities include the dessert <a href="/wiki/Bakewell_pudding" title="Bakewell pudding">Bakewell pudding</a>, very different from the nationally available <a href="/wiki/Bakewell_tart" title="Bakewell tart">Bakewell tart</a>, and until 2009 the famous cheese <a href="/wiki/Stilton_(cheese)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stilton (cheese)">Stilton</a> and other local cheeses were produced in the village of <a href="/wiki/Hartington,_Derbyshire" title="Hartington, Derbyshire">Hartington</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Activities">Activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mamtor-paragliding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mamtor-paragliding.jpg/220px-Mamtor-paragliding.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mamtor-paragliding.jpg/330px-Mamtor-paragliding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mamtor-paragliding.jpg/440px-Mamtor-paragliding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="926" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mamtor-paragliding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paragliding" title="Paragliding">Paragliding</a> from Mam Tor.</div></div></div>
<p>An extensive network of public footpaths and numerous long-distance trails, over 1,800 miles (2,900&#160;km) in total,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and large open-access areas are available for hillwalking and hiking.  The <a href="/wiki/Pennine_Way" title="Pennine Way">Pennine Way</a> traverses the Dark Peak from Edale to the park's northern boundary just south of <a href="/wiki/Standedge" title="Standedge">Standedge</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bridlepath" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridlepath">Bridleways</a> are used by <a href="/wiki/Mountain_biking" title="Mountain biking">mountain bikers</a>, as well as horse riders. The <a href="/wiki/Tissington_Trail" title="Tissington Trail">Tissington Trail</a> and <a href="/wiki/High_Peak_Trail" title="High Peak Trail">High Peak Trail</a>, which re-use former railway lines, are well used by walkers, horse riders and cyclists.<sup id="cite_ref-Tissington_Trail_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tissington_Trail-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sections of the former rail routes were converted to multi-purpose cycle trails. After the Woodhead Line was closed between <a href="/wiki/Hadfield,_Derbyshire" title="Hadfield, Derbyshire">Hadfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penistone" title="Penistone">Penistone</a> part of the trackbed was used for the <a href="/wiki/Longdendale_Trail" title="Longdendale Trail">Longdendale Trail</a> section of the <a href="/wiki/Trans_Pennine_Trail" title="Trans Pennine Trail">Trans Pennine Trail</a> between Hadfield and <a href="/wiki/Woodhead,_Derbyshire" title="Woodhead, Derbyshire">Woodhead</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The Manchester, Buxton, Matlock and Midlands Junction Railway between <a href="/wiki/Rowsley" title="Rowsley">Rowsley</a> and Buxton is part of the <a href="/wiki/Monsal_Trail" title="Monsal Trail">Monsal Trail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The trackbed of the Cromford and High Peak Railway is open to the public as the High Peak Trail.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The Tissington Trail uses another disused rail line between Buxton and Ashbourne<sup id="cite_ref-Tissington_Trail_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tissington_Trail-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Manifold_Way" title="Manifold Way">Manifold Way</a> utilises the <a href="/wiki/Leek_and_Manifold_Valley_Light_Railway" title="Leek and Manifold Valley Light Railway">former line</a> between <a href="/wiki/Waterhouses,_Staffordshire" title="Waterhouses, Staffordshire">Waterhouses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hulme_End" title="Hulme End">Hulme End</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Local authorities run cycle hire centres at Ashbourne, <a href="/wiki/Parsley_Hay" class="mw-redirect" title="Parsley Hay">Parsley Hay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middleton-by-Wirksworth" title="Middleton-by-Wirksworth">Middleton Top</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Derwent_Valley" title="Upper Derwent Valley">Upper Derwent Valley</a> and Hulme End.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Wheelchair access is possible at several places on the former railway trails, and cycle hire centres offer vehicles adapted to wheelchair users.<sup id="cite_ref-Tissington_Trail_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tissington_Trail-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> A project to make footpaths more accessible to less-agile walkers has replaced stiles with gates.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gritstone outcrops at Stanage Edge and The Roaches, are recognised as some of the finest <a href="/wiki/Rock_climbing" title="Rock climbing">rock climbing</a> sites in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Trekking_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trekking-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Limestone is more unstable but provides many testing climbs. <a href="/wiki/Thor%27s_Cave" title="Thor&#39;s Cave">Thor's Cave</a> was explored in the early 1950s by <a href="/wiki/Joe_Brown_(climber)" title="Joe Brown (climber)">Joe Brown</a> and others. Eleven limestone routes there are listed by the <a href="/wiki/British_Mountaineering_Council" title="British Mountaineering Council">BMC</a>, ranging in <a href="/wiki/Grade_(climbing)" title="Grade (climbing)">grade</a> from Very Severe to E7, and several more have been claimed since the guidebook's publication; a few routes are bolted.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Caving" title="Caving">Caving</a> takes place in the natural caves, potholes and old mine workings found in the limestone of the White Peak. Peak Cavern, the largest and most important cave system, is even linked to the <a href="/wiki/Speedwell_Cavern" title="Speedwell Cavern">Speedwell</a> system at Winnats. The largest potholes are Eldon Hole and Nettle Pot. Many old mine workings were often extensions of natural cave systems. They can be found at Castleton, Winnats, Matlock, <a href="/wiki/Stoney_Middleton" title="Stoney Middleton">Stoney Middleton</a>, Eyam, <a href="/wiki/Monyash" title="Monyash">Monyash</a> and Buxton.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reservoirs such as <a href="/wiki/Torside_Reservoir" title="Torside Reservoir">Torside Reservoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damflask_Reservoir" title="Damflask Reservoir">Damflask Reservoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carsington_Water" title="Carsington Water">Carsington Water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Lake" title="Rudyard Lake">Rudyard Lake</a> are centres for <a href="/wiki/List_of_water_sports" title="List of water sports">water sports</a>, including sailing, fishing and <a href="/wiki/Canoeing" title="Canoeing">canoeing</a>. Other activities include air sports such as <a href="/wiki/Hang_gliding" title="Hang gliding">hang gliding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paragliding" title="Paragliding">paragliding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fell_running" title="Fell running">fell running</a>, <a href="/wiki/Off-roading" title="Off-roading">off-roading</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orienteering" title="Orienteering">orienteering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Trekking_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trekking-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg/170px-Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg/255px-Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg/340px-Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="836" data-file-height="1240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peak_District_National_Park_tunnels.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing tunnels beneath the Peak District</div></div></div>
<p>The first roads constructed by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Romans</a> may have followed existing tracks. The Roman network  linked the settlements and forts of Aquae Arnemetiae (Buxton), <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield" title="Chesterfield">Chesterfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ardotalia" title="Ardotalia">Ardotalia</a> (Glossop) and Navio (<a href="/wiki/Brough_and_Shatton" title="Brough and Shatton">Brough and Shatton</a>), and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Parts of the modern <a href="/wiki/A515_road" title="A515 road">A515</a> and <a href="/wiki/A53_road" title="A53 road">A53</a> roads south of Buxton are believed to follow the routes of Roman roads.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Packhorse" title="Packhorse">Packhorse</a> routes criss-crossed the Peak in the Medieval era, and some paved causeways such as the Long Causeway along <a href="/wiki/Stanage_Edge" title="Stanage Edge">Stanage Edge</a> date from this period.  No highways were marked on <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Saxton" title="Christopher Saxton">Christopher Saxton</a>'s 1579 map of Derbyshire.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Bridge-building improved the transport network.
</p><p>Although the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">turnpike roads</a> from 1731<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> reduced journey times, the journey from Sheffield to Manchester in 1800 still took 16 hours prompting <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a> to remark that "a tortoise could outgallop us!"<sup id="cite_ref-Stainforth_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stainforth-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> From around 1815, better quality turnpike roads were built. The <a href="/wiki/Snake_Pass" title="Snake Pass">Snake Pass</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/A57_road" title="A57 road">A57</a>, was built under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Telford" title="Thomas Telford">Thomas Telford</a> in 1819–21.<sup id="cite_ref-Stainforth_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stainforth-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The main roads are the A57 between Sheffield and Manchester, the <a href="/wiki/A628_road" title="A628 road">A628</a>, Woodhead Pass, between <a href="/wiki/Barnsley" title="Barnsley">Barnsley</a> and Manchester via Longdendale, the <a href="/wiki/A6_road_(England)" title="A6 road (England)">A6</a> from Derby to Manchester via Buxton, the <a href="/wiki/Cat_and_Fiddle_Road" title="Cat and Fiddle Road">Cat and Fiddle Road</a> from Macclesfield to Buxton, and in the north, the <a href="/wiki/A635_road" title="A635 road">A635</a> from Manchester to Barnsley. Roads and lanes are frequently congested and the availability of parking spaces in towns and villages is a significant problem, especially during summer. A congestion charge was proposed in 2005, but was rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cromford_Canal" title="Cromford Canal">Cromford Canal</a> opened in 1794, to carry coal, lead and iron ore to the <a href="/wiki/Erewash_Canal" title="Erewash Canal">Erewash Canal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> it was abandoned in 1944. The canal turnpike roads had to compete with railways after 1825.<sup id="cite_ref-Stainforth_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stainforth-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The first railway, the <a href="/wiki/Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway" title="Cromford and High Peak Railway">Cromford and High Peak Railway</a> from <a href="/wiki/High_Peak_Junction" title="High Peak Junction">High Peak Junction</a> to <a href="/wiki/Whaley_Bridge" title="Whaley Bridge">Whaley Bridge</a>, was an industrial line, but passenger services  followed, including the <a href="/wiki/Woodhead_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodhead Line">Woodhead Line</a> (Sheffield to Manchester via Longdendale) and the <a href="/wiki/Manchester,_Buxton,_Matlock_and_Midlands_Junction_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester, Buxton, Matlock and Midlands Junction Railway">Manchester, Buxton, Matlock and Midlands Junction Railway</a>. Several rail lines were closed in the 1960s as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Beeching_Axe" class="mw-redirect" title="Beeching Axe">Beeching Axe</a>. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_transport">Public transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Public transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg/170px-Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg/255px-Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg/340px-Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Totley_Tunnel_western_portal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Totley_Tunnel" title="Totley Tunnel">Totley Tunnel</a> on the Manchester to Sheffield line.</div></div></div>
<p>The Peak District is accessible by public transport. Train services operate along the <a href="/wiki/Hope_Valley_line" title="Hope Valley line">Hope Valley line</a> between Sheffield and Manchester, the <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Valley_line" title="Derwent Valley line">Derwent Valley line</a> between Derby and <a href="/wiki/Matlock,_Derbyshire" title="Matlock, Derbyshire">Matlock</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Buxton_line" title="Buxton line">Buxton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glossop_line" title="Glossop line">Glossop</a> lines, linking them to Manchester, and the <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_line" title="Huddersfield line">Huddersfield line</a> from Manchester to Huddersfield.<sup id="cite_ref-Public_Transport_–_Peak_District_National_Park_Authority_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Public_Transport_–_Peak_District_National_Park_Authority-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Coach services provide access to Matlock, Bakewell and Buxton from Derby, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a> and Manchester through <a href="/wiki/High_Peak_(bus_company)" class="mw-redirect" title="High Peak (bus company)">TransPeak</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Express_Coaches" title="National Express Coaches">National Express</a>, and there are regular buses from Sheffield, Glossop, Stoke, <a href="/wiki/Leek,_Staffordshire" title="Leek, Staffordshire">Leek</a> and Chesterfield. The nearest airports are <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport" title="Manchester Airport">Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doncaster_Sheffield_Airport" title="Doncaster Sheffield Airport">Doncaster Sheffield</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Airport" title="East Midlands Airport">East Midlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Public_Transport_–_Peak_District_National_Park_Authority_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Public_Transport_–_Peak_District_National_Park_Authority-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In rural areas, villages are served by internal transport links. Minibuses operate from the towns to smaller villages. The Hope Valley and Buxton Line trains serve stations at  Hathersage, Hope and Edale.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cycle England invested £1.25&#160;million to build and improve cycle routes in the national park for leisure and commuting.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_issues">Conservation issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Conservation issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Derwent_walkers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Derwent_walkers.jpg/220px-Derwent_walkers.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Derwent_walkers.jpg/330px-Derwent_walkers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Derwent_walkers.jpg/440px-Derwent_walkers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Derwent_walkers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walkers above the <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Reservoir_(Derbyshire)" title="Derwent Reservoir (Derbyshire)">Derwent Reservoir</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The proximity of the Peak to major conurbations and urban areas (an estimated 20 million people live within an hour's drive)<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> poses challenges for managing the area. The park authority, the National Trust and other landowners attempt to keep the upland landscape accessible for recreation while protecting it from intensive farming, <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> and pressure from visitors. Tension exists between the needs of the 38,000 residents,<sup id="cite_ref-Place_called_home_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Place_called_home-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> the needs of the millions of people who visit,<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and conservation requirements.
</p><p>The uneven distribution of visitors creates stresses. Dovedale alone receives about two million visitors annually.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Peak_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Peak-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Other highly visited areas include Bakewell, Castleton and the <a href="/wiki/Hope_Valley,_Derbyshire" title="Hope Valley, Derbyshire">Hope Valley</a>, Chatsworth, Hartington and the reservoirs of the Upper Derwent Valley.<sup id="cite_ref-Tourism_factsheet_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tourism_factsheet-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> More than 60% of visits take place between May and September, with Sunday the busiest day.<sup id="cite_ref-Tourism_factsheet_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tourism_factsheet-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Footpath users in the more popular walking areas have contributed to serious erosion problems, particularly on the fragile <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> moorlands. The use of some paths by mountain bikers is believed to have exacerbated the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Measures to contain the damage include the diversion of the official route of the Pennine Way out of Edale, which now goes via Jacob's Ladder rather than following the Grindsbrook, and surfacing moorland footpaths with expensive natural stone paving.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Some rights of way have been damaged by legal and illegal use by off-road vehicles such as <a href="/wiki/Four-wheel_drive" title="Four-wheel drive">4×4s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trail_bike" class="mw-redirect" title="Trail bike">trail bikes</a>. Campaigners have sought to reduce their impact.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Large-scale limestone quarrying is contentious. Most mineral extraction licences were issued by national government for 90 years in the 1950s and remain legally binding. The park authority has a policy of considering applications for new quarries and licence renewal in the park in terms of the local and national need for the mineral and how unique the source is, in conjunction with the effects on traffic,  residents and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Mineral_factsheet_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mineral_factsheet-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Some licences have not been renewed; the RMC Aggregates quarry at <a href="/wiki/Eldon_Hill" title="Eldon Hill">Eldon Hill</a> closed in 1999 and landscaped.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Proposals from Stancliffe Stone Ltd to re-open dormant gritstone quarries at Stanton Moor in 1999 became a test case, hotly contested by ecological protesters and residents on the grounds that the development would threaten Bronze Age remains at the Nine Ladies Stone Circle, and damage the natural landscape.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, negotiations took place to relocate the development to Dale View quarry in a less sensitive area.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Peak_District_in_literature_and_arts">Peak District in literature and arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Peak District in literature and arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The landscapes of the Peak have inspired writers for centuries. Various places have been identified by Ralph Elliott and others as locations in the 14th-century poem <i><a href="/wiki/Sir_Gawain_and_the_Green_Knight" title="Sir Gawain and the Green Knight">Sir Gawain and the Green Knight</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Lud%27s_Church" title="Lud&#39;s Church">Lud's Church</a> is thought to be the <a href="/wiki/Green_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Chapel">Green Chapel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg/220px-Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg/330px-Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg/440px-Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chatsworth_House" title="Chatsworth House">Chatsworth House</a> – The setting for a recent adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Key scenes in <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>'s 1813 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i> are set in the Peak.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
<i><a href="/wiki/Peveril_of_the_Peak" title="Peveril of the Peak">Peveril of the Peak</a></i> (1823) by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Sir Walter Scott</a> is a historical novel set at Peveril Castle, Castleton during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a> was a frequent visitor to Matlock; the Peak inspired several of his poems, including an 1830 <a href="/wiki/Sonnet" title="Sonnet">sonnet</a> to Chatsworth House.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Experience_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Experience-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The village of <a href="/wiki/Morton,_Derbyshire" title="Morton, Derbyshire">Morton</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Bront%C3%AB" title="Charlotte Brontë">Charlotte Brontë</a>'s 1847 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre">Jane Eyre</a></i> is based on Hathersage, where Brontë stayed in 1845, and <a href="/wiki/Thornfield_Hall" title="Thornfield Hall">Thornfield Hall</a> might have been inspired by nearby North Lees Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Snowfield in <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>'s first novel <i><a href="/wiki/Adam_Bede" title="Adam Bede">Adam Bede</a></i> (1859) is believed to be based on Wirksworth, where her uncle managed a mill; <a href="/wiki/Ellastone" title="Ellastone">Ellastone</a> (as Hayslope) and <a href="/wiki/Ashbourne,_Derbyshire" title="Ashbourne, Derbyshire">Ashbourne</a> (as Oakbourne) also feature.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Experience_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Experience-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>, the author of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Rabbit" title="Peter Rabbit">Peter Rabbit</a>, visited her uncle <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Potter" title="Edmund Potter">Edmund Potter</a> at his printworks in <a href="/wiki/Dinting_Vale" title="Dinting Vale">Dinting Vale</a>. She copied cloth samples from his pattern book to dress her characters. Mrs Tiggywinkle's shawl, in <a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Mrs._Tiggy-Winkle" title="The Tale of Mrs. Tiggy-Winkle">The Tale of Mrs. Tiggy-Winkle</a>, is based on pattern number 222714.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Children's author <a href="/wiki/Alison_Uttley" title="Alison Uttley">Alison Uttley</a> (1884–1976) was born at <a href="/wiki/Cromford" title="Cromford">Cromford</a>; her novel <i>A Traveller in Time</i>, set in <a href="/wiki/Dethick,_Lea_and_Holloway" title="Dethick, Lea and Holloway">Dethick</a>, recounts the <a href="/wiki/Babington_Plot" title="Babington Plot">Babington Plot</a> to free <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, from imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-Peakland_Heritage_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peakland_Heritage-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crichton_Porteous" title="Crichton Porteous">Crichton Porteous</a> (1901–91) set several books in locations in the Peak; <i>Toad Hole</i>, <i>Lucky Columbell</i> and <i>Broken River</i> are set in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Derwent_Valley" title="Upper Derwent Valley">Derwent Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geraldine_Brooks_(writer)" title="Geraldine Brooks (writer)">Geraldine Brooks</a>'s first novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Year_of_Wonders" title="Year of Wonders">Year of Wonders</a></i> (2001), blends fact and fiction to tell the story of the plague village of Eyam.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Eyam also inspired <i>Children of Winter</i> by children's novelist <a href="/wiki/Berlie_Doherty" title="Berlie Doherty">Berlie Doherty</a> who has set several other works in the Peak, including <i>Deep Secret</i>, based on the drowning of the villages of <a href="/wiki/Derwent,_Derbyshire" title="Derwent, Derbyshire">Derwent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashopton" title="Ashopton">Ashopton</a> by the Ladybower Reservoir, and <i>Blue John</i>, inspired by the cavern at Castleton.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ladybower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ladybower.jpg/220px-Ladybower.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ladybower.jpg/330px-Ladybower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ladybower.jpg/440px-Ladybower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ladybower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ladybower_Reservoir" title="Ladybower Reservoir">Ladybower Reservoir</a> in the Upper Derwent Valley, location for filming <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dam_Busters_(film)" title="The Dam Busters (film)">The Dam Busters</a></i></div></div></div><p>Many works of crime and horror have been set in the Peak. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Terror_of_Blue_John_Gap" title="The Terror of Blue John Gap">The Terror of Blue John Gap</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Sir Arthur Conan Doyle</a> (1859–1930) recounts terrible events at the Blue John mines,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</a> investigates the kidnapping of a child in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventure_of_the_Priory_School" title="The Adventure of the Priory School">The Adventure of the Priory School</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Many horror stories by local author <a href="/wiki/Robert_Murray_Gilchrist" title="Robert Murray Gilchrist">Robert Murray Gilchrist</a> feature Peak settings.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Experience_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Experience-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Booth_(writer)" title="Stephen Booth (writer)">Stephen Booth</a> has written a series of crime novels set in real and imagined Peak locations,<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> while <i>In Pursuit of the Proper Sinner</i>, an <a href="/wiki/The_Inspector_Lynley_Mysteries" title="The Inspector Lynley Mysteries">Inspector Lynley mystery</a> by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_George" title="Elizabeth George">Elizabeth George</a>, is set on the fictional Calder Moor.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other writers and poets who lived in or visited the Peak include <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Congreve" title="William Congreve">William Congreve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Seward" title="Anna Seward">Anna Seward</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Gordon_Byron,_6th_Baron_Byron" class="mw-redirect" title="George Gordon Byron, 6th Baron Byron">Lord Byron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Moore" title="Thomas Moore">Thomas Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Furness" title="Richard Furness">Richard Furness</a>, <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vera_Brittain" title="Vera Brittain">Vera Brittain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richmal_Crompton" title="Richmal Crompton">Richmal Crompton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Gould" class="mw-redirect" title="Nathaniel Gould">Nat Gould</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peak_Experience_157-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peak_Experience-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peakland_Heritage_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peakland_Heritage-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The landscapes and historic houses are popular settings for film and television. The classic 1955 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dam_Busters_(film)" title="The Dam Busters (film)">The Dam Busters</a></i> was filmed at the Upper <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Reservoir_(Derbyshire)" title="Derwent Reservoir (Derbyshire)">Derwent Valley reservoirs</a>, where practice flights for the bombing raids on the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr_(river)" title="Ruhr (river)">Ruhr</a> dams had been made during the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In adaptations of <i>Pride and Prejudice</i>, <a href="/wiki/Longnor,_Staffordshire" title="Longnor, Staffordshire">Longnor</a> has featured as Lambton, and Lyme Park and Chatsworth House have stood in for <a href="/wiki/Pemberley" title="Pemberley">Pemberley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Haddon Hall doubled as <a href="/wiki/Thornfield_Hall" title="Thornfield Hall">Thornfield Hall</a> in two different adaptations of <i>Jane Eyre</i> and has appeared in several films including <i><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_(film)" title="Elizabeth (film)">Elizabeth</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Princess_Bride_(film)" title="The Princess Bride (film)">The Princess Bride</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Other_Boleyn_Girl_(2008_film)" title="The Other Boleyn Girl (2008 film)">The Other Boleyn Girl</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The television medical drama <i><a href="/wiki/Peak_Practice" title="Peak Practice">Peak Practice</a></i> set in the fictional village of Cardale was filmed in <a href="/wiki/Crich" title="Crich">Crich</a>, Matlock and other Peak locations.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The world's longest-running sitcom, <i><a href="/wiki/Last_of_the_Summer_Wine" title="Last of the Summer Wine">Last of the Summer Wine</a></i>, was filmed in <a href="/wiki/Holmfirth" title="Holmfirth">Holmfirth</a> and the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Holme_Valley" title="Holme Valley">Holme Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
